    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="b7c9f1002d1a442f86474957fff3323d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b7c9f1002d1a442f86474957fff3323d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '08b9ddc7-cefa-4a22-a00c-924921fa6fff'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Material Processing and Die and Mold Technology, School of Materials Science and Engineering, Huazhong University of Science and Technology, Wuhan, 430074 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Li/Zhen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Luo/Wei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Wei Luo</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d151287699" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries are competitive candidates for large-scale energy storage batteries due to the abundant sodium resource. However, the electrode interface in the conventional electrolyte is unstable, deteriorating the cycle life of the cells. Introducing functional electrolyte additives can generate stable electrode interfaces. Here, pentafluoro(phenoxy)cyclotriphosphazene (FPPN) serves as a functional electrolyte additive to stabilize the interfaces of the layered oxide cathode and the hard carbon anode. The fluorine substituting groups and the π–π conjugated <i>─</i>PN<i>─</i> structure decrease the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital and increase the highest occupied molecular orbital of FPPN, respectively, realizing the preferential reduction and oxidization of FPPN on the anode and cathode simultaneously, which results in the formation of a uniform, ultrathin, and inorganic-rich solid electrolyte interlayer and cathode electrolyte interphase. The sodium-ion pouch cells of 5 Ah capacity rather than coin cells are assembled to evaluate the effect of FPPN. It can retain a high capacity of 4.46 Ah after 1000 cycles, corresponding to a low decay ratio of 0.01% per cycle. The pouch cell also achieves a high energy density of 145&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a wide operating temperature of −20–60&nbsp;°C. This work can attract more attention to the rational electrolyte design for practical applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202312287-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202312287-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have achieved remarkable progress in portable electronics, electric vehicles, and grid energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0001_R_d151287621e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, precious lithium (Li) resources hinder the further large-scale applications of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0002_R_d151287621e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0005_R_d151287621e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are an ideal substitute for LIBs for next-generation energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0006_R_d151287621e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today Energy">6</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0009_R_d151287621e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with lithium resources, sodium has enormous advantages of natural abundance and low cost. Furthermore, sodium (Na) cannot form an alloy with aluminum (Al), so Al foil can serve as the anode current collector to replace expansive and heavy copper (Cu) foil, which further improves the energy density and decreases the cost of SIBs;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0010_R_d151287621e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Mater. Lett.">10</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0013_R_d151287621e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, sodium ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) owns weaker Lewis acid than lithium-ion (Li<sup>+</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0014_R_d151287621e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202312287-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0015_R_d151287621e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> so that the desolvation process (rate-determining step) in SIBs is faster than in LIBs, leading to excellent rate performance of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0016_R_d151287621e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>So far, there are three main kinds of cathodes for SIBs: polyanions, layered oxides, and Prussian blue. Among them, layered oxides own higher operating voltage than polyanions and better cycling stability than Prussian blue, showing a tremendous competitive edge. Hard carbon (HC) is almost the only option for the anode at this stage due to its high reversible capacity of around 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0017_R_d151287621e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, both the layered oxide cathode and the HC anode still suffer from unstable electrolyte/electrode interfaces, which seriously limit the cycling stability of the batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0018_R_d151287621e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Battery Energy">18</a>, <a href="#adma202312287-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0019_R_d151287621e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Energy Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The transition metal elements in the layered oxides, such as nickel and manganese, will likely be dissolved into electrolytes and deposited on the HC anode, increasing the interfacial resistance. Besides, for the anode, the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation plateau of hard carbon is only ≈0.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), so Na metal plating on the hard carbon surface occurs quickly when the current density is high. Thus, constructing stable electrode–electrolyte interfaces simultaneously at the cathode and anode side is the key to realizing long-cycle SIBs. Similar to the LIBs, the stability of the electrode interface heavily depends on the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) and the solid electrolyte interlayer on the anode (SEI). However, the stability of CEI and SEI of SIBs is poorer than in LIBs. The main reason is that the crucial species, such as NaF, have much higher solubility in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0010_R_d151287621e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Mater. Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the naturally formed CEI and SEI cannot limit the subsequent parasitic reactions between the electrolytes and electrodes, gradually consuming the Na resource and electrolyte. The CEI and SEI can be improved by tuning the electrolyte's components, such as introducing new salts, new solvents, and functional additives, which can affect the chemical elements and the structure distribution of the interlayer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0020_R_d151287621e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0023_R_d151287621e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, Sun et&nbsp;al. reported succinic anhydride (SA) as a filming additive to improve the lifespan of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0024_R_d151287621e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, SA can guide a uniform and stable CEI containing more oxygen-rich organic species, reducing the side reactions at the interface. As a result, capacity retention is increased from 54.6% to 89.9% after 150 cycles at 0.2 C. Li et&nbsp;al. used <i>N</i>-phenyl-bis(trifluoromethanesulfonimide) (PTFSI) as a functional additive to optimize the SEI on the hard carbon (HC) surface, which improves the capacity retention of Na/HC half cells from 0% to 68% after 500 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0025_R_d151287621e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Res.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So far, the research in SIBs mainly focuses on electrode materials, and the CEI and SEI regulation has not drawn enough attention. On the other hand, the performance evaluation in the current research is mainly based on coin cells (mAh-level) with excess electrolyte infiltration and Na inventory source.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0024_R_d151287621e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#adma202312287-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0026_R_d151287621e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0030_R_d151287621e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Some issues, such as gas generation during cycles, are always neglected in a coin cell. At the same time, it can be easily observed in a pouch cell. Thus, evaluating electrolytes in the Ah-level pouch cells is more reliable. The generated gas inside cells can be divided into two types. One is the gas from the electrolyte oxidized decomposition on the cathode surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0008_R_d151287621e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Energy Lett.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the high cutoff charging voltage of 4.0&nbsp;V, the electrolyte can be oxidized, generating oxygen-containing gas, such as carbon dioxide. It is worth noting that the cathode surface includes many transition metal (Ni, Fe, Mn) elements. These elements may catalyze the oxidized decomposition of electrolytes during charging. Second is the electrolyte reduction decomposition gas at the hard carbon surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0020_R_d151287621e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a>-<a href="#adma202312287-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0022_R_d151287621e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the specific surface of the hard carbon in sodium batteries is higher than the graphite in lithium batteries, which means that more electrolytes are reduced to SEI, and more gas is generated. In addition, the Na plating quickly occurs. The active Na metal can react with electrolytes directly, releasing some alkane. All in all, the gas is generated in the electrode/electrolyte interfaces. Thus, manipulating the chemical components of electrode/electrolyte interfaces is crucial to suppress gas generation.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we propose pentafluoro(phenoxy)cyclotriphosphazene (FPPN) as a multifunctional electrolyte additive to optimize CEI and SEI simultaneously. Beneficial from its F substituting groups and the π–π conjugated <i>─</i>P═N<i>─</i> phosphonitrile ring, FPPN can be reduced and oxidized preferentially at anode and cathode surfaces, respectively, promoting the formation of sodium fluoride (NaF) in SEI and CEI. The NaF-rich CEI and SEI can stabilize the interface structure and suppress side reactions between the electrolyte and electrode. Pouch full cells (5 Ah) rather than coin cells are assembled to evaluate the electrochemical performance: the pouch cell with FPPN addition delivers a remarkable capacity retention of 88.9% after 1000 cycles. The FPPN-supported pouch cells also achieve an energy density of 145&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the cell) and an expanded operation temperature (−20–60&nbsp;°C). The gas generation from the interfacial side reactions is also suppressed effectively. Besides, the pouch cells pass the nail penetration test, showing a high safety performance. Thus, the research enables Ah-level pouch cells to have remarkable comprehensive properties, which provides a new additive for the electrolyte design for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202312287-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202312287-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0001">1</a> shows the function mechanism of FPPN. The plain electrolyte for sodium-ion is prepared with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>)&nbsp;dissolved in ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1/1/1, v/v), and 5 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), which is the most widely used formula. Compared with EC, PC, DEC, and FEC, the susceptive FPPN molecules are more accessible to be oxidized on the surface of the layered NaNi<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<i><sub>z</sub></i>O (NNFMO) cathode and be reduced on the surface of the hard carbon (HC) anode during the initial charging process, resulting in the NaF-rich CEI and SEI due to its high F density. NaF has high electronic conductivity and excellent mechanical strength, widely considered a vital interphase component to promise stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9b214142-a881-468d-92c8-e4f4f0b41a52/adma202312287-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9b214142-a881-468d-92c8-e4f4f0b41a52/adma202312287-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6d842635-847d-4a78-8909-071b996eb398/adma202312287-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9b214142-a881-468d-92c8-e4f4f0b41a52/adma202312287-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A schematic illustration of the FPPN electrolyte guides the stable CEI and SEI.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To design the electrolyte rationally, theoretical calculations of different molecules and ionic clusters are conducted, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2</a>. The highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) of molecules represent the thermodynamical potential of oxidation and reduction, respectively. Lower LUMO suggests the molecule is easily reduced and forms SEI at the anode surface, while higher HOMO means the molecule is likely to be oxidated at the cathode side to form CEI. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2a</a>, the HOMO and LUMO levels of ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), and FPPN are calculated as −7.03/−0.48, −6.91/−0.46, −7.50/−0.93, −6.62/−0.08, and −6.72/−1.78&nbsp;eV, respectively. Even coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup>, HOMO and LUMO levels both decrease: −11.37/−5.17 of Na<sup>+</sup>-EC, −11.22/−5.12 of Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, −12.08/−5.32 of Na<sup>+</sup>-FEC, −12.88/−5.45 of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEC, and −9.99/−5.35&nbsp;eV of Na<sup>+</sup>-FPPN (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2b</a>). Thus, the FPPN prefers to be oxidized at the cathode surface and reduced at the anode surface thermodynamically. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2c</a> shows different electrostatic potentials (ESP) of molecules, which indicates the solvation ability of various solvents. It can be seen the O atoms in all the molecules show negative charge densities, suggesting Na<sup>+</sup> prefers to connect with the O atom. The O atom in FPPN has the highest electrostatic potential of −0.031&nbsp;eV, indicating the weakest solvation ability. The binding energies of Na<sup>+</sup> and various solvents are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2d</a>: −1.42, −1.45, −1.25, −1.15, and −0.86&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-EC, Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, Na<sup>+</sup>-FEC, Na<sup>+</sup>-DEC, and Na<sup>+</sup>-FPPN, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2d</a>). The charge density difference is also calculated to determine the relationship between Na<sup>+</sup> and molecules (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2e</a>). Fewer electron transference indicates weaker coordination. The ESP, binding energy, and charge density results show that FPPN does not coordinate with Na<sup>+</sup> and that the bulk electrolyte will retain intrinsic chemical properties.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a2990ee-a084-486e-a9d9-a8a845ff9d67/adma202312287-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a2990ee-a084-486e-a9d9-a8a845ff9d67/adma202312287-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8474ebd3-d82e-4d62-bf57-0d46af9cf009/adma202312287-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a2990ee-a084-486e-a9d9-a8a845ff9d67/adma202312287-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculations of different molecules and ionic clusters. a) HOMO and LUMO levels. b) ESP of EC, PC, FEC, DEC, and FPPN. c) Binding energies and d) Electron density difference of Na<sup>+</sup>-EC, Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, Na<sup>+</sup>-FEC, Na<sup>+</sup>-DEC, and Na<sup>+</sup>-FPPN.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The solvating structure of Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk electrolytes is simulated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3a,d</a>. In the baseline electrolyte, some sharp peaks of Na-O<sub>EC</sub>, Na-O<sub>PC</sub>, Na-O<sub>FEC</sub>, Na-O<sub>DEC</sub>, and Na-F<sub>PF6</sub> appear around 2.29 Å, while Na-O<sub>DEC</sub> and Na-F<sub>PF6</sub> peaks appear at 4.01 and 4.45 Å, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3b,e</a>). The results indicate the organic solvent molecules occupy the primary solvating shell, and partial anions are in the second shell in the baseline electrolyte. According to the coordination numbers calculated by the radial distribution function (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3c,f</a>), the solvation unit of Na<sup>+</sup> in the baseline electrolyte can be written as Na<sup>+</sup>[EC]<sub>1.45</sub>[PC]<sub>1.44</sub>[FEC]<sub>0.12</sub>[DEC]<sub>0.75</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>0.54</sub>. After introducing FPPN, the intensities of Na-O<sub>PC</sub> and Na-O<sub>DEC</sub> peaks decrease while the intensity of Na-O<sub>FEC</sub> increases, which is beneficial to developing a NaF-rich CEI and SEI. Due to the weak solvation ability, FPPN is in the outer shell (&gt;5 Å). Thus, the solvation unit of Na<sup>+</sup> in the FPPN electrolyte is recorded as Na<sup>+</sup>[EC]<sub>1.48</sub>[PC]<sub>1.30</sub>[FEC]<sub>0.17</sub>[DEC]<sub>0.69</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>0.55</sub>[FPPN]<sub>0.001</sub>. For the Na<sup>+</sup> transport process in the bulk electrolyte, the ionic conductivities are almost unchanged from 7.86 to 7.26 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> after adding FPPN in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3g</a>. The FT-IR spectrum shows the chemical state of electrolytes is also unchanged after introducing FPPN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The FPPN additive does not change the bulk structure of the electrolytes. The electrochemical windows of electrolytes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3h,i</a>. The sharp peak at about 0.6&nbsp;V represents the solvent reduction process. After introducing FPPN, the peak shifts to lower potential, and the maximum current density decreases, too, indicating that FPPN inhibits solvent reduction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3h</a>). In addition, the oxidation current of electrolytes at high voltage (&gt; 4&nbsp;V) is reduced after FPPN addition, which is ascribed to the CEI forming boosted by the preferential oxidation of FPPN, which suppresses the further oxidation of the solvents (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0003">3i</a>). Thus, the FPPN additive can significantly reduce the parasitic reactions at both the anode and cathode sides and thus improve the interface stability of the cathode and anode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0abd1c8-637f-44ce-ad81-9a5108e653dc/adma202312287-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0abd1c8-637f-44ce-ad81-9a5108e653dc/adma202312287-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d285863e-d764-4147-aec3-98c70ea894f6/adma202312287-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0abd1c8-637f-44ce-ad81-9a5108e653dc/adma202312287-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Molecular dynamic simulations of the baseline and FPPN electrolytes: a) snapshot, b) radiation distribution function, and c) coordination number of the baseline electrolyte. d) Snapshot, e) radiation distribution function, and f) coordination number of the FPPN electrolyte. g) The ionic conductivities and h,i) linear scan voltammetry of the baseline and FPPN electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The NNFMO/HC pouch cells (5 Ah) are assembled to investigate the effect of the FPPN additive on the electrochemical performances (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The fabrication parameters of pouch cells are displayed in (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). All manufacturing parameters are close to application requirements: high areal mass loading of 20 mg<sub>NNFMO</sub> cm<sup>−2</sup>, low negative/positive capacity ratio of 1.1, and low electrolyte/capacity ratio of 5&nbsp;g Ah<sup>−1</sup>. The crystal structures and morphologies of the electrode materials (NNFMO and HC) are shown in (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3S6</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structure of NNFMO belongs to the R3m (166) space group, while the HC is amorphous (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The sizes of NNFMO and HC are 5–10 and 3–5&nbsp;µm, respectively (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4 and S5</a>, Supporting Information). Transition metal elements (Ni, Fe, and Mn) are distributed evenly in the particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The initial discharge curves show small individual differences between different cells (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information compares the electrochemical performance of the NNFMO/HC pouch cells with different FPPN additions. The NNFMO/HC cell with 2 wt% FPPN electrolyte delivers an initial capacity of 4.79 Ah. It keeps 4.63 Ah after 100 cycles at a 1.0 C rate, corresponding to a remarkable capacity retention of 96.7%, which is much higher than those with other electrolytes (87.7% of the baseline electrolyte, 87.1%, and 94.5% for the electrolytes with 1 wt% and 5 wt% FPPN additive). The initial Coulombic efficiencies are 79.6%, 81.6%, 81.7%, and 81.2 wt% for the pouch cells with the baseline electrolyte, the electrolytes with 1 wt% FPPN, 2 wt% FPPN, and 5 wt% FPPN, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The charge/discharge curves of cells in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information show the voltage hysteresis is reduced after adding FPPN, confirming the FPPN can manipulate CEI and SEI and thus facilitate the reaction kinetics. Fewer FPPN additives (1 wt%) contribute to nonuniform CEI and SEI. Higher FPPN additives (5 wt%) may increase the thickness of CEI and SEI, which is not beneficial to the Na<sup>+</sup> transference in the SEI. In addition, the ionic conductivity of 5 wt% FPPN (6.13 S cm<sup>−1</sup>) is lower than that of 2 wt% FPPN (7.28 S cm<sup>−1</sup>) in (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, the most suitable FPPN addition is 2 wt%. The rate performance of pouch cells with 2 wt% FPPN is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0004">4a–c</a>. The discharge capacities of the pouch cell with FPPN are 5.06, 4.96, 4.86, 4.74, and 4.65 Ah at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 3 C, respectively, much higher than those of the cell with the baseline electrolyte: 4.84, 4.73, 4.61, 4.49, and 4.41 Ah at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 3 C, respectively. The pouch cell with FPPN also shows great improvement in cycling stability. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0004">4d</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information, the pouch cell with FPPN can deliver an initial capacity of 5.02 and maintain 4.46 Ah at 0.5 C after 1000 cycles, corresponding to a low average capacity decay ratio of 0.01% per cycle. In contrast, the pouch cell with the baseline electrolyte only delivers an initial capacity of 4.85 and keeps 4.43 Ah after 260 cycles. The gravimetric and volumetric energy densities of the pouch cell with FPPN are as high as 145&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and 258&nbsp;Wh L<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), which can charge the smartphone typically (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Even without any pressure, the pouch cell with FPPN still demonstrates a higher discharge capacity of 4.4 Ah after 100 cycles than baseline (3.8 Ah) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The result confirms that FPPN still has a positive effect even without pressure. The electrochemical performance at high and low temperatures is also crucial to the applications. When the temperature increases to 60&nbsp;°C, the pouch cell without FPPN additive shows rapid capacity decay during 20 cycles because the side reactions are more serious, and the interfaces become unstable with increased temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0004">4e</a>). In contrast, the cell with the FPPN additive realizes stable cycling during 120 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C. When the working temperature decreases to 0 and −20&nbsp;°C, the pouch cell with FPPN additive shows discharge capacities of 3.93 and 3.78 Ah at 0.5C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0004">4f</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), much higher than those with the baseline electrolyte (3.18 and 3.02 Ah). When the temperature is further decreased to −40 °C, the cell with FPPN can show a higher discharge capacity of 3.0 Ah at 0.2C than baseline of 2.7 Ah (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The pouch cell with FPPN shows smaller polarization during charging than the baseline cell at 0 and −20&nbsp;°C, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The self-discharge test is also conducted to monitor the interfacial stability. The open-circuit voltage (OCV) of the fully charged (4&nbsp;V) pouch cells with baseline electrolyte drastically reduces to 3.73&nbsp;V after 88 days of aging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the OCV of the pouch cell with the FPPN additive remains at 3.78&nbsp;V after the same aging time. The self-discharge test at a high temperature is necessary for a wider range of applications. When the temperature increases to 40&nbsp;°C, the cell's open circuit voltage with FPPN is maintained at 3.82&nbsp;V after 200&nbsp;h. In sharp contrast, the cell's open circuit voltage without FPPN drops dramatically to 3.80&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, Supporting Information). Ultrasonic imaging technology is adopted to observe the gas generation visually inside the pouch cells. Generally, unstable CEI and SEI cannot limit the side reactions at the electrolyte electrode interface. The side reactions always produce gas, but gas generation is difficult to observe in coin cells due to the abundant dead cavities. The gassing problem can be well investigated in the pouch cell system. Here, the ultrasonic image is a red-to-blue map in which fewer ultrasonic signs correspond to the blue color, implying severe gassing behavior. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0004">4g</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information, the pouch cell with the baseline electrolyte continuously generates gas during cycling. At the 200th cycle, the map becomes blue, demonstrating severe side reactions accompanied by gas generation. In sharp contrast, the pouch cell with the FPPN electrolyte displays suppressed gas generation, and no obvious blue signs appear after 200 cycles. Due to serious gas generation, the thickness of pouch cells without FPPN is increased from 10.25 to 17.55&nbsp;mm after 200 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). However, the thickness of the cell with FPPN still maintains 10.81&nbsp;mm. Beneficial from the preferential oxidation and reduction reactions of FPPN, the cathode/electrolyte and anode/electrolyte are stable. The stable electrode/electrolyte interfaces can isolate the contact between electrolytes and electrolytes, inhibiting the electrolyte oxidation and reduction, confirmed by the LSV results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0002">2h,i</a>. The inorganic-rich SEI can accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> transference, effectively alleviating the Na deposition behavior. The results confirm that FPPN can boost NaF-rich CEI and SEI formation, effectively suppressing the interface side reactions and improving the electrode kinetics. The surface temperature of cells is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information. When the cell charges, the surface temperature is increased because many parasitic reactions can produce much heat. Thus, more side reactions lead to higher surface temperatures. It is necessary to control the temperature increase for applications. The infrared imaging technique is adopted to monitor the surface temperature during charging. At 2C charging, the surface temperature of the pouch cell without FPPN is gradually increased to 37.8 °C. In contrast, the maximum surface temperature of the cell with FPPN is only 35.5 °C. The above results confirm that the FPPN additive can effectively reduce parasitic reactions inside pouch cells.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e9c6265-ba69-41af-8672-70051c067d87/adma202312287-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e9c6265-ba69-41af-8672-70051c067d87/adma202312287-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/121e2edf-6194-4c58-8369-064f99470b43/adma202312287-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e9c6265-ba69-41af-8672-70051c067d87/adma202312287-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance and ultrasonic images of 5 Ah NNFMO/HC pouch cells. a) Rate performance and b,c) charging/discharging curves of pouch cells with the baseline and FPPN electrolytes. d) Long cycling stability test of pouch cells at 0.5 C. e) Cycling stability at a high temperature of 60&nbsp;°C. f) The discharge comparison at a low temperature of −20&nbsp;°C. g) Ultrasonic images of pouch cells with the baseline and FPPN electrolytes at 1st, 50th, 100th, 150th, and 200th cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To get further insight into the functional mechanism of FPPN on the cathode side, postmortem characterizations are carried out for improved electrochemical performance after adding FPPN. From the morphologies of the NNFMO cathodes detected by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM), it can be seen the particles of the pristine cathode show a smooth surface (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0005">5a</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a</a>, Supporting Information). After 200 cycles, cracks and pores can be observed on the NNFMO electrode in the baseline electrolyte. At the same time, the smooth morphology of the NNFMO is well maintained in the FPPN system (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23b,c</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, from the TEM images, we can see the CEI layer in the baseline electrolyte is 37&nbsp;nm thick (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0005">5b</a>), while the CEI layer is only 18&nbsp;nm in the FPPN electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0005">5c</a>). The thicker CEI arises from persistent electrolyte decomposition. The chemical components of the CEI layer after cycling are studied by X-ray photoelectron microscopy (XPS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0005">5d,e</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). The peaks at 284.8, 285.6, 286.8, 289.3, and 290.9&nbsp;eV in the C 1s spectra are ascribed to C<i>─</i>C, C<i>─</i>O, C═O, O═C<i>─</i>O<i>─</i>R, and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> bonds. The 684.8 and 688.1&nbsp;eV peaks in the F 1s spectra belong to Na<i>─</i>F and C<i>─</i>F bonds, respectively. The polar O═C<i>─</i>O<i>─</i>R species can promise flexibility and compactness, while the Na<i>─</i>F phase can accelerate ionic diffusion in the CEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0031_R_d151287621e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#adma202312287-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0032_R_d151287621e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> FPPN can increase the O═C<i>─</i>O<i>─</i>R and Na<i>─</i>F species ratio, developing better CEI. In addition, NaPO<i><sub>x</sub></i>F<i><sub>y</sub></i> and NaPF<i><sub>x</sub></i>, which may be the CEI corrosion conduct due to some harmful agents, such as HF, are observed in the baseline electrolyte, while absent in the FPPN electrolyte, further suggesting that the stability of the FPPN-derived CEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0019_R_d151287621e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Energy Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the <sup>19</sup>F nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectrum, much PO<i><sub>x</sub></i>F<i><sub>y</sub></i> and HF is monitored in cycled baseline electrolyte but absent in the FPPN electrolyte, which confirms FPPN can eliminate corrosive species during cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with other research.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202312287-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0033_R_d151287621e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ChemElectroChem">33</a>, <a href="#adma202312287-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202312287-bib-0034_R_d151287621e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A higher P2p atomic ratio in the baseline electrolyte confirms the server corrosion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e32f330b-f8b6-4c71-a590-a38c141bc265/adma202312287-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e32f330b-f8b6-4c71-a590-a38c141bc265/adma202312287-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/470a5d22-d893-4dc6-a1a7-adfac5dafce1/adma202312287-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e32f330b-f8b6-4c71-a590-a38c141bc265/adma202312287-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of NNFMO cathodes a) pristine and cycled in the b) baseline and c) FPPN electrolytes. XPS spectra of NNFMO electrodes in d) baseline and e) FPPN electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) is conducted for the NNFMO cathode cycling at 0.5 C with a 2–4&nbsp;V voltage range, as shown in (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0006">6</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). For the two NNFMO cathodes in the electrolyte with/without FPPN during the charging process, the (003) and (006) peaks shift to lower angle degrees. In contrast, the (101), (012), and (104) peaks turn to higher angle degrees, demonstrating the <i>c</i>-axis expansion and the reconstruction of the <i>ab</i>-plane during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. By comparison, the shift degree of the (003) central peak in the FPPN system is lower (1.5°) than that in the baseline electrolyte (2.1°). In the following discharging process, all peaks remain in the initial position, indicating the excellent reversibility of NNFMO. The FPPN-derived CEI promotes the structural stability of NNFMO during cycling.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e087b47f-ba1f-44bd-b990-c79363962e85/adma202312287-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e087b47f-ba1f-44bd-b990-c79363962e85/adma202312287-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6ce70fed-9e89-49bd-a780-4d72c66d1851/adma202312287-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e087b47f-ba1f-44bd-b990-c79363962e85/adma202312287-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The in situ X-ray diffraction test of NNFMO cathodes in the baseline and FPPN electrolytes: a) Discharging/charging curves and b) in situ XRD results during cycling of the cell with the baseline electrolyte. c) Discharging/charging curves and b) in situ XRD results during cycling of the cell with the FPPN electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>On the anode side, FPPN boosts the formation of a uniform and dense SEI, effectively preventing electrode corrosion and electrolyte depletion during long-term cycling. The organic species are almost identical in the baseline and FPPN electrolytes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202312287-fig-0007">7d,e</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). However, the SEI in the FPPN electrolyte contains more Na-F and fewer C-F components. Similarly, the NaPO<i><sub>x</sub></i>F<i><sub>y</sub></i> and NaPF<i><sub>x</sub></i> peaks are also observed in the P 2p spectra after cycling in the baseline electrolyte. In contrast, almost no P2p signs are detected in the FPPN electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). The TEM images show a nonuniform layer covering the HC electrode after cycling in the baseline electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0007">7a,b</a>). In contrast, the HC particles cycled in the FPPN electrolyte deliver a compact surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202312287-fig-0007">7c</a>). These above results indicate that FPPN can promote the formation of uniform, ultrathin, and compact CEI and SEI. Besides, the pouch cell with the FPPN additive also demonstrates much better safety: the pouch cell with the baseline electrolyte immediately leads to a fearful thermal runaway under nail penetration (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30a and S31a</a>, Supporting Information), while the pouch cell with the FPPN additive shows regular without any deformation (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30b and S31b</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information, some white particles were observed on the cycled HC electrode in the baseline electrolyte. At the same time, the HC electrode cycled in the FPPN electrolyte remains brownish. According to the SEM images, noodle-like Na metal dendrites are observed in the baseline electrolyte, while the HC maintains a smooth surface in the FPPN electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information). Element mappings confirm many Na elements and some transition metal elements, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information. The improved safety performance is ascribed to three reasons: 1) suppressed gas evolution. The generating gas inside cells is usually an incentive. FPPN can effectively reduce the gas evolution, promising safety performance. 2) Absent Na plating. After introducing FPPN, Na plating is inhibited. However, many Na dendrites are observed in the baseline electrolyte. The deposited Na is porous and quickly reacts with air, which produces many combustible gases, such as hydrogen. 3) Scavenged free radicals. The <i>─</i>P═N<i>─</i> molecules can usually scavenge free radicals in the thermal runaway. Thus, the thermal runway path is avoided.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202312287-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e35946bb-e797-4528-b8af-30a08b6ea80b/adma202312287-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e35946bb-e797-4528-b8af-30a08b6ea80b/adma202312287-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca349058-747c-48fb-bb24-a4f2e6afc40b/adma202312287-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e35946bb-e797-4528-b8af-30a08b6ea80b/adma202312287-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202312287-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202312287" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of HC anodes a) pristine and cycled in the b) baseline and c) FPPN electrolytes. XPS spectra of HC electrodes in d) baseline and e) FPPN electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202312287-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202312287-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a dual-functional electrolyte additive of FPPN is proposed to stabilize CEI and SEI for 5 Ah sodium-ion pouch full cells. The FPPN additive promotes the content of NaF in CEI and SEI simultaneously. Thus, the parasitic reactions between the electrodes and electrolytes are inhibited effectively. The 5 Ah NNFMO/HC pouch cell with 2 wt% FPPN achieves a high energy density of 145&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a stable cycle, maintaining 4.46 Ah after 1000 cycles at 0.5 C, vastly exceeding the baseline system of 4.43 Ah after 260 cycles. The NNFMO/HC pouch cell with 2 wt% FPPN also demonstrates a wide operating temperature from −20 to 60&nbsp;°C. Moreover, the pouch cell with FPPN delivers enhanced safety performance at nail penetration. The fundamental findings presented in this research provide meaningful guidance for rationally manipulating CEI and SEI in Ah-level pouch cells and bridging the gap between interfacial chemistry and electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adma202312287-bib-0002"><span class="bullet">2</span><span class="author">A. Rudola</span>, <span class="author">R. Sayers</span>, <span class="author">C. J.
